Herein, the one-dimensional (1D) porous FeS/carbon fibers (FeS/CFs) micro/nanostructures are fabricated through facile pyrolysis of double-helix-structured Fe-carrageenan fibers. The FeS nanoparticles are in situ formed by interacting with sulfur-containing group of natural material ι-carrageenan and uniformly embedded in the unique 1D porous carbon fibrous matrix, significantly enhancing the sodium-ion storage performance. The obtained FeS/CFs with optimized sodium storage performance benefits from the appropriate carbon content (20.9 wt %). The composite exhibits high capacity and excellent cycling stability (283 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles) and rate performance (247 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). This work provides a simple strategy to construct 1D porous FeS/CFs micro/nanostructures as high-performance anode materials for SIBs via a unique sustainable and environmentally friendly way.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D17175%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DDaohao%2BLi%252C%2BYuanyuan%2BSun%252C%2BShuai%2BChen%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D10%26issueNum%3D20%26contentID%3Dacsami.8b03059%26title%3DHighly%2BPorous%2BFeS%252FCarbon%2BFibers%2BDerived%2Bfrom%2BFe-Carrageenan%2BBiomass%253A%2BHigh-capacity%2Band%2BDurable%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D17182%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2018" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2018 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290882&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Fibers"> Fibers </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292333&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Porosity"> Porosity </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291675&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Stability"> Stability </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Double-helix+Structures&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_double-helix%20structures">double-helix structures</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fe-carrageenan+Biomass&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Fe-carrageenan%20biomass">Fe-carrageenan biomass</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fes%2Fcarbon+Fibers&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_FeS%2Fcarbon%20fibers">FeS/carbon fibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20materials">anode materials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with the advantages of geographical distribution of sodium and cost effectiveness have been attracting extensive attention. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Ideal SIBs should be featured with long-term cycling life and high energy density. Developing high-performance electrode materials with high specific capacities and excellent rate performances and cycling stability is of great significance for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3−5)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">For SIB anode materials, the high-theoretical-specific-capacity materials, such as Sn, Sb, P, metal sulfides (MSs), etc., were reported as promising candidates. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> Among them, the MSs, such as FeS<sub>2</sub>, CoS<sub>2</sub>, SnS<sub>2</sub>, and MoS<sub>2</sub>, have been successfully employed as anode materials for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 9 to 14">(9−14)</a> Unfortunately, MSs usually suffer from unsatisfactory electronic conductivity and poor structural stability during Na<sup>+</sup> de/insertion reaction process, leading to poor rate performance and cycling stability. Construction of core/shell-structured carbon-encapsulated MS nanoparticle (NP) composites is an effective way to enhance the well-designed structure, which can not only enhance the electrons transport but also maintain the structural stability of the MS-based active materials during a charge/discharge process, consequently resulting in enhanced cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 15 to 19">(15−19)</a> However, some studies have reported that an excess of carbon content in the MS/C composites results in decreased active material content and reduced sodium-ion transport capacity, leading to inferior low specific capacities and rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20−22)</a> Apparently, designing optimal content carbon-encapsulated MS NP composites would additionally promote the electrolyte ions’ diffusion to enhance capacities and rate performance.</div><div class="NLM_p">The carbon-based composites with one-dimensional (1D) porous micro/nanostructures can demonstrate significant impacts for energy storage, achieving high capacity and outstanding rate performance and cycling properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> The 1D continuous structure can offer interconnected electron transport channels through active materials to the current collector. Besides, the 1D porous composites can provide large surface area and appropriate pore size distribution, which allow for high contact area of the electrode to electrolyte to shorten the electrolyte ions’ diffusion distance. Furthermore, the small-sized active material NPs in a 1D micro/nanostructure could be accommodated in the volume expansion to restrict degradation of NPs during cycling. Nevertheless, the traditional methods for fabricating such 1D porous MSs/C micro/nanostructures are usually either complicated or use nonrenewable petrochemical resources and environmentally unfriendly sulfides (such as H<sub>2</sub>S) as precursors. Thus, the newly green and sustainable method must be developed to manufacture optimal carbon content 1D porous MSs/C micro/nanostructures as a high-capacity and durable anode for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Recently, the renewable and abundant biomass resources, especially the more available and potential seaweed biomass, have been used as precursors or template for fabricating the energy storage materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 25 to 27">(25−27)</a> Herein, we used the Fe-carrageenan biomass as precursor to prepare 1D porous and core/shell-structured FeS NP (25–45 nm)/carbon fiber (a diameter of about 10–15 μm) composites (FeS/CFs). The Fe-carrageenan fibers were prepared through wet-spinning carrageenan using the coagulating bath containing Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions. Carrageenan macromolecules undergo cross-linking with Fe<sup>3+</sup> through forming double-helix structures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28−30)</a> After pyrolysis of the Fe-carrageenan fibers, the structures of sulfate groups binding Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions are converted to FeS NPs without any additional sulfur source and the carrageenan macromolecules are decomposed into H<sub>2</sub>O, CO, CO<sub>2</sub>, and a spot of SO<sub>2</sub> to generate a porous structure and finally transformed into porous carbon, forming 1D porous FeS/CF micro/nanostructures. The confinement effect of the double-helix structure for Fe<sup>3+</sup> can lead to uniform distribution of the FeS NPs in the 1D porous carbonaceous microfibers with core/shell structure. A carbon removal process was carried out using CO<sub>2</sub> at 600 °C with different removal times <i>t</i> h to obtain FeS/CFs-<i>t</i> (<i>t</i> = 1 and 2). We found the FeS/CFs-1 with 20.9 wt % carbon content exhibited high reversible capacities, excellent rate performance, and cycling stability (283 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles, 247 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The ι-carrageenan was provided by the Qingdao Haizhilin Biotechnology Development Limited Company. Iron(III) chloride hexahydrate (FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O) was purchased from Sinopharm Chemical Reagent (Shanghai, China).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Preparation of FeS/CFs-<i>t</i></h3><div class="NLM_p last">The 7.0 wt % ι-carrageenan solution was prepared at 80 °C and made homogeneous with deionized water under high-speed stirring, and then this solution was extruded from a spinneret into a coagulating bath containing 3.0 wt % FeCl<sub>3</sub> ethanol solution to form Fe-carrageenan fibers. The obtained Fe-carrageenan fibers were washed with distilled water and dried. The Fe-carrageenan fibers were pyrolyzed at 800 °C at the heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> for 1 h in Ar atmosphere to obtain FeS/CFs. Finally, the FeS/CFs were exposed to calcination at 600 °C at a heating rate of 7 °C min<sup>–1</sup> for t h in CO<sub>2</sub> atmosphere to obtain FeS/CFs-<i>t</i> (<i>t</i> = 1 and 2).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Coupled thermogravimetric and mass spectrum (TG-MS) were obtained using Evolution 16/18 and OMNI star instruments. The phase structures were characterized with X-ray diffraction (XRD, DX2700, China) operating with Cu Kα radiation (<i>l</i> = 1.5418 Å) at a scan rate (2θ) of 1° min<sup>–1</sup>, with the accelerating voltage of 40 kV. The specific surface area was calculated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method from the data in a relative pressure (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>) range between 0.05 and 0.25, and pore structures were characterized. The chemical composition was investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) using an ESCALab250 electron spectrometer (Thermo Scientific Corporation) with monochromatic 150 W Al Kα radiation. The morphologies and structures of the samples were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM; SU8020) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and a high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) (FEI Tecnai G20).</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The samples were mixed with carbon black and poly(vinylidene difluoride) in a weight ratio of 80:10:10 in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone. The slurry was coated on the Cu foil and dried for 10 h at 120 °C under vacuum. The 2016 type coin cells were assembled in an Ar-filled glovebox. Sodium metal was used as the reference electrode and counter electrode, and the separator was Whatman GF/D. The electrolyte was 1.0 M solution of NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (1:1 vol %) containing 5 wt % fluoroethylene carbonate. The electrochemical performances were tested in a voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na using a cell testing instrument (LAND CT2001A and CHI 760E). The specific capacities and current densities are based on the mass of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-<i>t</i>.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.1.  Synthesis of the FeS/Carbon Fibers</h3><div class="NLM_p">As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>, a wet-spinning process was used to fabricate biomass microfibers. First, the ι-carrageenan solution (prepared at a temperature of 80 °C) was extruded from a spinneret into a coagulating bath containing Fe<sup>3+</sup> ethanol solution to form Fe-carrageenan fibers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). In this step, random coil carrageenan macromolecules would gelate with Fe<sup>3+</sup>. The Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions not only assist in the formation of a double-helix structure but also promote the aggregation of different double helices to form junction zones, which enable long-range cross-linking. The weight percentage of Fe<sup>3+</sup> species in Fe-carrageenan fibers was measured to be about 16.4 wt % using thermogravimetric (TG) analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). After the facile pyrolysis process of Fe-carrageenan fibers in inert atmosphere, the organic and inorganic Fe-carrageenan fibers were converted to FeS/CFs. Finally, the FeS/CFs-<i>t</i> (<i>t</i> = 1 and 2) were obtained through the calcination process at 600 °C in CO<sub>2</sub> atmosphere for <i>t</i> h.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0008.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of FeS/CFs-<i>t</i>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.2.  Characterizations of the FeS/Carbon Fibers and the Precursors</h3><div class="NLM_p">To research the decomposition mechanism of Fe-carrageenan fibers during the pyrolysis process, the coupled thermogravimetric and mass spectrum (TG-MS) analysis was executed. The TG curve presents that the whole process of thermal decomposition for the Fe-carrageenan fibers between 50 and 700 °C with 70.9 wt % weight loss is the result of decomposition of carrageenan macromolecules and the structures of sulfate groups binding Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions are converted to FeS NPs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a). The derivative thermogram (DTG) curve shows two distinct weight losses at the temperatures of around 100–240 and 420–450 °C, corresponding to the fast thermal decompositions of carrageenan in these stages. The corresponding mass spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) illustrates the evolved gases are steam (H<sub>2</sub>O, <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 18), carbon monoxide (CO, <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 28), carbon dioxide (CO<sub>2</sub>, <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 44), and sulfur dioxide (SO<sub>2</sub>, <i>m</i>/<i>z</i> = 64). A mass of H<sub>2</sub>O and CO and a spot of SO<sub>2</sub> are evolved during the whole process of thermal decomposition, which are derived from the oxygen-containing groups and sulfate groups. The majority of sulfate groups interacting with Fe<sup>3+</sup> through ionic forces were gradually converted to FeS. The purple curve assigned to CO<sub>2</sub> has two peaks at around 170 and 460 °C, which is related to the two fast weight losses (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a), indicating the fast consumption of carbon by oxygen in precursor at these temperatures. To sum up, the whole process of thermal decomposition for the Fe-carrageenan fibers under Ar atmosphere is the result of decomposition of carrageenan macromolecular structure, where the oxygen-containing groups (−C–O–C–, −OH, and −SO<sub>4</sub>) were converted to H<sub>2</sub>O, CO, CO<sub>2</sub>, and SO<sub>2</sub> and the residue is FeS/CFs.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0004.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) TG (pink) and DTG (olive) curves of Fe-carrageenan fibers in Ar atmosphere (heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>). (b) The analysis of the outlet gases from the decomposition of Fe-carrageenan fibers TG-MS: H<sub>2</sub>O (pink), CO (olive), CO<sub>2</sub> (purple), and SO<sub>2</sub> (orange).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">X-ray diffraction (XRD) was used to measure the structures of the samples. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a, the strong and sharp diffraction peaks at 2θ values of 30.0, 33.8, 43.1, 53.2, and 71.3 are observed from the XRD patterns of all of the FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 samples, corresponding to the diffractions of (110), (112), (114), (310), and (224) planes, respectively, indicating the pure phase of FeS (JCPDF no. 00-037-0477) in the composites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> Comparing the XRD pattern of Fe-carrageenan fibers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), the Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions are completely converted to FeS species through pyrolysis in Ar atmosphere. In addition, the peak intensities of FeS and the amorphous carbon located at about 20–25° are increased and reduced with the increase of carbon removal time, respectively. The average diameters of FeS NPs in the samples were roughly estimated through Scherrer’s formula, which are 62, 59, and 102 nm for FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2, respectively. The carbon contents of the obtained FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 were determined to be about 35.7, 20.1, and 9.4 wt %, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The porous characteristics of the samples were also investigated. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, the FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-1 exhibit the typical type II adsorption isotherm and the absorption occurs at low relative pressure (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> &lt; 0.02), which corresponds to the adsorption in micropores. The micropores of the FeS/CFs should be attributed to the thermal decomposition of carrageenan macromolecules. However, there is no microporous adsorption for FeS/CFs-2. In addition, the hysteresis loops of all of the isotherms arise from the mesoporous structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area (<i>S</i><sub>BET</sub>) of FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 is 374, 265, and 78 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The density functional theory pore size distributions calculated from the adsorption branch of the isotherms exhibit that the FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-1 contain micro- and mesopores, whereas FeS/CFs-2 has small amount of micro- and mesoporous structures (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0005.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of FeS/CFs, FeS/CFS-1, and FeS/CFs-2. (b) Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherm and (c) the corresponding pore size distribution curves for FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement was carried out to research the detailed elemental composition of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-<i>t</i> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5</a>). The survey spectra show the presence of S, C, O, and Fe elements in all of the samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a). The peak intensity of Fe species is obviously increased through a carbon removal process, and the peak intensities of C and O are reduced. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5a,b</a>, all of the three high-resolution Fe 2p spectra can be decomposed into five peaks in the binding energy range of 706–730 eV. The two unfitted peaks at about 712 and 725 eV are assigned to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. The four fitted peaks at around 711.1, 713, 724.6, and 726.5 eV confirm the existence of Fe–S bond. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> The peak at 719.0 eV corresponds to the Fe–O bond, which may originate from the surface oxidation of samples in air. For S 2p spectra, the peaks located at 161.7 and 163.0 eV are ascribed to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, corresponding well with those of FeS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5c,d</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 34 and 35">(34,35)</a> The bonding of sulfur with oxygen (S–O bond) is supported by the peaks centered at 166.6 and 167.8 eV. In addition, the spectra of C 1s of all of the samples are fitted to four peaks, which can be assigned to C–C (284.4 eV), C═C (285.0 eV), C–O (286.0 eV), and C═O (288.5 eV) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5e,f</a>). The residual oxygen-containing groups (C–O, C═O, and S–O) in the samples are originated from the thermal decomposition of carrageenan macromolecule (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5g–i</a>, respectively).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0009.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XPS spectra of FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2. High-resolution XPS spectra of FeS/CFs-1, (b) Fe 2p spectrum, (c) S 2p spectrum, and (d) O 1s spectrum.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the samples was measured by scanning electron microscopy (SEM). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a, the Fe-carrageenan fibers are 1D longish and straight, with a diameter of about 20–27 μm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b, by statistical measurement using Nano Measurer software). A mass of wrinkles formed in the wet-spinning process exists on the surface of Fe-carrageenan fibers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). After pyrolysis of Fe-carrageenan fibers, the obtained FeS/CFs can retain their fibrous structure and show a rugged surface with a large shrinkage of their diameter (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d) and FeS/CFs-1 and FeS/CFs-2 with reduced carbon content exhibit a morphology similar to that of FeS/CFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e,f). The SEM energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) was used to analyze the elemental compositions of the samples. The C, Fe, and S elements are homogeneously distributed in the FeS/CFs-1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>g). In addition, the C element in FeS/CFs-2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) is obviously fewer than in the other two samples according to the EDS mapping for C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>), indicating more carbon being removed with the increase of carbon removal time. A large amount of macropores can be observed on the surface of the sample with a diameter of about 100 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>h), which can act as a reservoir for electrolyte to promote the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup>. In addition, the obtained 1D porous micro/nanostructure composites display a typical lotus-root-like porous structure (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>i), which may be due to the decomposition of most organic moieties of the Fe-carrageenan fibers through the pyrolysis process.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. SEM images of (a, c) Fe-carrageenan fibers, (d) FeS/CFs, (e) FeS/CFs-1, and (f) FeS/CFs-2. (b) The diameter distribution of the Fe-carrageenan fibers. (g) EDS mappings of FeS/CFs-1 for C, Fe, and S elements. (h) The high magnification and (i) cross-sectional SEM images of FeS/CFs-1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) further characterize the structure and component of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-<i>t</i>. Large amounts of FeS NPs are uniformly distributed in the FeS/CFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a). For FeS/CFs-1, the diameter of FeS NPs is similar to that of FeS/CFs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b) and the NPs are still well covered by a carbon layer. With the increase of carbon removal time, the size of FeS NPs increased, attributing to the aggregation of NPs with less carbon coated during the process of carbon removal (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c). The HRTEM image of FeS/CFs-1 exhibits a set of lattice fringes with a spacing of 0.208 nm corresponding to the (114) plane of FeS (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). Furthermore, abundant micropores are observed in the amorphous carbon surrounding the FeS NPs, which benefits the promotion of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion to enhance the energy storage performance.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0002.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. TEM images of (a) FeS/CFs, (b) FeS/CFs-1, and (c) FeS/CFs-2. (d) HRTEM image of FeS/CFs-1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.3.  Electrochemical Performance of the FeS/Carbon Fibers</h3><div class="NLM_p">The samples were used as anode materials for SIBs to evaluate the sodium storage performances by cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements. FeS undergoes the sodium storage performance through the redox reaction (FeS + 2Na<sup>+</sup> + 2e<sup>–</sup> → Na<sub>2</sub>S + Fe) with a theoretical capacity of about 609 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a> show the CV curves of FeS/CFs-1, FeS/CFs, and FeS/CFs-2 in the first three cycles with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the potential window of 0.01–3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). For the FeS/CFs-1, the cathodic reduction peaks located at 1.43, 0.87, and 0.37 V in the first cathodic scan are attributed to the electrochemical reduction of FeS with Na<sup>+</sup> to form the Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe and the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. These peaks shift to 1.54, 0.91, and 0.27 V in the two subsequent cathodic scans, which correspond to sodiation processes. For the anodic scans, the peaks located at 1.34, 1.78, and 2.11 V demonstrate transition of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe to Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>FeS and Na<sub>2</sub>FeS<sub>2</sub> species during the desodiation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> In addition, the pair of oxidation/reductive peaks appear in 0.10 and 0.03, corresponding to the extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> to carbon, respectively. The redox peaks in CV curves of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-2 are similar to those of FeS/CFs-1, and the initial cathodic scans of the three samples are distinct attributed to the different carbon contents and porous structure. The second and third CV curves of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-1 show no obvious change, indicating good stability. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b exhibits the galvanostatic charge/discharge curves of FeS/CFs-1 anode at the current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. For the first discharge curve, the plateaus at around about 0.9 and 0.6 V correspond to the conversion reaction of FeS to Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S and these plateaus in subsequent discharge curves shift to about 0.9–1.3 and 0.35 V. Two charge platforms at 1.3 and 1.8 V are found in all charge curves. In addition, the first discharge and charge specific capacities of FeS/CFs-1 are 460 and 317 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, with a Coulombic efficiency of 68.9%, respectively. The irreversible capacity during the first cycle is mainly attributed to formation of SEI on the surface of FeS/CFs-1 and the irreversible insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0003.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) CV curves of FeS/CFs-1 in the first three cycles. (b) The charge and discharge curves of FeS/CFs-1 at the first, second, and third cycle between 0.01 and 3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) The specific capacities and cycling performances of FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) The specific capacity of FeS/CF-1 at different current densities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The specific capacities and long cycling stability of carbon fibers without FeS NPs (CFs), FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 were evaluated. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c, the reversible charge capacity of CFs is only about 75 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which suggested that such pure carbon material is not a high-performance anode material for SIBs. After compositing with FeS NPs, the reversible charge capacity of FeS/CFs-1 can attain 283 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, exhibiting obvious advantage over CFs and FeS/CFs (207 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). For FeS/CFs-2, the specific capacity fades seriously after 100 cycles, although it exhibits high specific capacity at the initial cycles. The high specific capacity and excellent cycling stability of the FeS/CFs-1 anode result from the optimal content of carbon in the composite (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The sodium storage performance of the FeS/CFs-1 electrode is compared to that of the reported iron sulfide-based electrodes, indicating its high specific capacity and cycling stability (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). The rate performance of the FeS/CFs-1 is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d. The charge specific capacities are 438, 376, 332, 303, 280, and 247 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns back to 5 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles, the charge specific capacity is recovered to 431 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the excellent rate capability of FeS/CFs-1.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) is further measured to evaluate the electrochemical performance of CFs, FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 (the fitted same equivalent circuit shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a, the Nyquist plots of the CF, FeS/CF, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 electrodes before the initial charge/discharge process show the typical semicircle at the high-frequency and a linear slope at low-frequency region. The semicircle, high-frequency intercept at the <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> axis, and the inclined line illustrate the charge-transfer resistance of the interface between electrodes and electrolyte (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), resistance of the electrolyte and other cell components (<i>R</i><sub>e</sub>), and the Warburg impedance (<i>W</i>), respectively. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of CFs, FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 electrodes are determined to be 202.2, 272.9, 253.4, and 627.3 Ω, respectively. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of FeS/CFs-2 with the unsatisfactory porous carbon structure is much larger than that of CFs, FeS/CFs, and FeS/CFs-1, suggesting the porous carbon-coated FeS NPs can decrease the resistance of the electrodes and promote Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and charge transfer. EIS can be devoted to calculate the values of Na<sup>+</sup>-ion diffusion coefficient <i>( D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>), using the following <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>A</i>, <i>C</i>, <i>F</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>R</i>, <i>n</i>, and σ represent the surface area of the electrode, concentration of Na<sup>+</sup>, Faraday constant, absolute temperature, gas constant, number of electrons per molecule during the redox process, and Warburg factor, respectively. The values of σ can be estimated from low-frequency inclined lines using the equation −<i>Z</i><sub>im</sub> = σω<sup>–1/2</sup>, where ω is the angular frequency. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b exhibits the relation between −<i>Z</i><sub>im</sub> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> through linear fitting of the spots. The FeS/CFs-1 exhibits a smaller σ value than that of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-2, resulting in the higher <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> calculated from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>. The order of <i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> is the same as that of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, further indicating that the appropriate carbon-coated FeS NPs are favorable for promoting the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/medium/am-2018-030594_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Nyquist plots of CF, FeS/CF, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 electrodes. (b) The relationship between −<i>Z</i><sub>im</sub> and the reciprocal square root of the angular frequency in the low-frequency region. Schematic illustration of Na<sup>+</sup> de/insertion reaction process of (c) FeS/CFs-1 and (d) FeS/CFs-2.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/asset/images/large/am-2018-030594_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b03059&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The FeS/CFs-1 anode for SIBs exhibits excellent sodium storage performance due to its distinctive 1D porous micro/nanostructure. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c, the carbonaceous microfiber matrix can retain the structural stability of FeS NPs during the charge/discharge process, which is crucial for promoting cycling stability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> For the unsatisfactory carbon content and large sizes of FeS NPs in FeS/CFs-2, the serious volume change of FeS after the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion could cause serious agglomeration, pulverization, and the loss of active materials from the electrodes, resulting in a poor cycling performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>d). In addition, the porous structure of FeS/CFs-1 can store the electrolyte as the “reservoirs”, increasing the interface between active materials and electrolyte, shortening the diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> and restraining the intermediate polysulfide dissolution into electrolyte during the charge/discharge process to prevent the loss of active substance. Furthermore, the 1D structure can serve as the channel for fast electron transport to promote the electrochemical conductivity of the anode, enhancing the rate performance.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, double-helix-structured Fe-carrageenan fibers were used to fabricate 1D porous FeS NPs/carbonaceous microfiber micro/nanostructures. The composite with core/shell structure was in situ formed in the pyrolysis process of Fe-carrageenan fibers without any addition of toxic sulfur source. The nanoconfined iron ions in the unique double-helix structure can ensure that the FeS NPs are uniformly embedded in the 1D porous carbon fibrous matrix. When evaluated as anode material for SIBs, the carbon-coated FeS NPs can effectively improve the structural stability of FeS NPs during Na<sup>+</sup> de/insertion reaction process, guaranteeing excellent cycling stability. The 1D porous carbon fibrous matrix can enhance ion and electron transport kinetics to improve the rate performance. The FeS/CFs-1 with 20.9 wt % carbon content as anode for SIBs exhibits high reversible capacities and excellent cycling stability (283 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 400 cycles) and rate performance (247 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Such a sustainable biomass conversion strategy provides a facile, green, and effective route for fabricating high-performance metal sulfide-based anode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b03059" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b03059</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Wet-spinning process for the preparation of Fe-carrageenan fibers (Figure S1); TG curve of Fe-carrageenan fibers in air (Figure S2); XRD pattern of Fe-carrageenan fibers (Figure S3); TG curves of FeS/CFs, FeS/CFs-1, and FeS/CFs-2 in air (Figure S4); XPS spectra of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-2 (Figure S5); FESEM images of of FeS/CFs-2 (Figure S6) and FeS/CFs (Figure S7) and the corresponding EDS mappings for C, Fe, and S elements; CV curves of FeS/CFs and FeS/CFs-2 (Figure S8); comparison of electrochemical performance between FeS/CFs-1 and recently reported high-performance iron sulfide-based electrodes (Figure S9), the fitted same equivalent circuit of the EIS measurement (Figure S10) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b03059/suppl_file/am8b03059_si_001.pdf">am8b03059_si_001.pdf (1.4 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b03059" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1016/j.cossms.2012.04.002"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, B.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Owing to almost unmatched volumetric energy d., Li-ion batteries have dominated the portable electronics industry and solid state electrochem. literature for the past 20 years.  Not only will that continue, but they are also now powering plug-in hybrid elec. vehicles and elec. vehicles.  In light of possible concerns over rising lithium costs in the future, Na and Na-ion batteries have re-emerged as candidates for medium and large-scale stationary energy storage, esp. as a result of heightened interest in renewable energy sources that provide intermittent power which needs to be load-levelled.  The sodium-ion battery field presents many solid state materials design challenges, and rising to that call in the past couple of years, several reports of new sodium-ion technologies and electrode materials have surfaced.  These range from high-temp. air electrodes to new layered oxides, polyanion-based materials, carbons and other insertion materials for sodium-ion batteries, many of which hold promise for future sodium-based energy storage applications.  In this article, the challenges of current high-temp. sodium technologies including Na-S and Na-NiCl2 and new molten sodium technol., Na-O2 are summarized.  Recent advancements in pos. and neg. electrode materials suitable for Na-ion and hybrid Na/Li-ion cells are reviewed, along with the prospects for future developments.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201410376"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kundu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Talaie, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Duffort, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nazar, L.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">36</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5343-5364</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201402511"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ha, K.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">FeS@C on Carbon Cloth as Flexible Electrode for Both Lithium and Sodium Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Xiang; Li, Weihan; Shi, Jin-an; Gu, Lin; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flexible and self-supported carbon-coated FeS on carbon cloth films (denoted as FeS@C/carbon cloth) is prepd. by a facial hydrothermal method combined with a carbonization treatment.  The FeS@C/carbon cloth could be directly used as electrodes for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (NIBs).  The synthetic effects of the structure, highly electron-conductive of carbon cloth, porous structure for electrolyte access, and uniform carbon shell on FeS surface to accommodate the vol. change lead to improved cyclability and rate capability.  For lithium storage, the FeS@C/carbon cloth electrode delivers a high discharge capacity of 420 mAh g-1 even after 100 cycles at a c.d. of 0.15 C and 370 mAh g-1at a high c.d. of 7.5 C (1 C = 609 mA g-1).  When used for sodium storage, it keeps a reversible capacity of 365 mAh g-1after 100 cycles at 0.15 C.  Similar process can be utilized for the formation of various cathode and anode composites on carbon cloth for flexible energy storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201503015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Tin Nanodots Encapsulated in Porous Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Nanofibers as a Free-Standing Anode for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tin Nanodots Encapsulated in Porous Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Nanofibers as a Free-Standing Anode for Advanced Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Zhang, Ning; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">42</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6702-6707</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ultra-small Sn nanodots (1-2 nm) were delicately encapsulated in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers through electrospinning and thermal treatment.  The size and content of Sn particles can be controlled by changing reaction conditions.  When evaluated as binder- and current collector-free anodes for rechargeable Na-ion batteries, Sn NDs@PNC nanofibers delivered a reversible capacity of 633 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  More impressively, a capacity of 450 mA h g-1 even at 10 000 mA g-1 and 483 mA h g-1 over 1300 cycles at 2000 mA g-1 were achieved.  To our knowledge, this is the highest rate performance and cyclic stability of tin for Na storage.  The exceptional electrochem. performance of Sn NDs@PNC is attributed to the following: First, the ultra-small Sn NDs (1-2 nm) decreases the abs. strain and improve the utilization rate of active materials during sodiation/de-sodiation process; second, the uniform distribution of Sn NDs in the fibrous carbon matrix is an important factor to generate a balanced stress over the whole composite with maintaining the integrity of the electrode upon prolonged cycling; third, the 1D porous N-doped carbon frame can interlink into a 3D conductive network, which facilitates the electron and Na+ diffusion kinetics and buffers the large vol. fluctuation of Sn NDs and prevents their pulverization and aggregation.  Carbon fibers also serve as both current collector and conductive material - this avoids the common loss of electrochem. contact between the active materials and the current collector/conductive additives, and reduces the inactive wt. and vol. of cells thus improving the energy d.  This simple and scalable synthesis as well as the promising electrochem. performance encourages application of Sn-based materials as anodes for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA00623F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Primary Li/FeS2 batteries have been commercialized and rechargeable Li-alloy/FeS2 batteries have been successfully operated at 375-500° by employing LiCl-based molten electrolytes.  However, non-aq. electrolyte rechargeable Li/FeS2 batteries have not yet been fulfilled due to poor reversibility and rapid capacity fading.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 Nanoflowers with Expanded Interlayers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Zhe; Wang, Lixiu; Zhang, Kai; Wang, Jianbin; Cheng, Fangyi; Tao, Zhanliang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">53</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayer spacing of the (002) plane were synthesized and used as high-performance anode in Na-ion batteries.  By controlling the cut-off voltage to the range of 0.4-3 V, an intercalation mechanism rather than a conversion reaction is taking place.  The MoS2 nanoflower electrode shows high discharge capacities of 350 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1, 300 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1, and 195 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1.  An initial capacity increase with cycling is caused by peeling off MoS2 layers, which produces more active sites for Na+ storage.  The stripping of MoS2 layers occurring in charge/discharge cycling contributes to the enhanced kinetics and low energy barrier for the intercalation of Na+ ions.  The electrochem. reaction is mainly controlled by the capacitive process, which facilitates the high-rate capability.  Therefore, MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayers hold promise for rechargeable Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b01003"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cui, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In Situ Fabrication of CoS and NiS Nanomaterials Anchored on Reduced Graphene Oxide for Reversible sodium Storage</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Facile synthesis and electrochemical sodium storage of CoS2 micro/nano-structures</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Xue; Zhang, Kai; Lei, Kaixiang; Li, Fujun; Tao, Zhanliang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">198-206</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRAEB5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1998-0000</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer GmbH</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the synthesis and electrochem. sodium storage of cobalt disulfide (CoS2) with various micro/nano-structures.  CoS2 with microscale sizes are either assembled by nanoparticles (P-CoS2) via a facile solvothermal route or nanooctahedrons constructed solid (O-CoS2) and hollow microstructures (H-CoS2) fabricated by hydrothermal methods.  Among three morphologies, H-CoS2 exhibits the largest discharge capacities and best rate performance as anode of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Furthermore, H-CoS2 delivers a capacity of 690 mA·h·g-1 at 1 A·g-1 after 100 cycles in a potential range of 0.1-3.0 V, and ∼240 mA·h·g-1 over 800 cycles in the potential window of 1.0-3.0 V.  This cycling difference mainly lies in the two discharge plateaus obsd. in 0.1-3.0 V and one discharge plateau in 1.0-3.0 V.  To interpret the reactions, X-ray diffraction (XRD) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) are applied.  The results show that at the first plateau around 1.4 V, the insertion reaction (CoS2 + xNa+ + xe- → NaxCoS2) occurs; while at the second plateau around 0.6 V, the conversion reaction (NaxCoS2 + (4 - x) Na+ + (4 - x)e- → Co + 2Na2S) takes place.  This provides insights for electrochem. sodium storage of CoS2 as the anode of SIBs.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Co9S8-carbon composite as anode materials with improved Na-storage performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ko, You Na; Kang, Yun Chan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis and electrochem. performance of a composite of Co9S8 nanoparticles and amorphous carbon is studied as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  The Co9S8-carbon composite powder was fabricated through a one-pot spray pyrolysis process using thiourea and polyvinylpyrrolidone as sulfur and carbon sources, resp.  The Co9S8 nanoparticles are entirely covered by an amorphous carbon layer.  The initial discharge and charge capacities of the Co9S8-carbon composite powder were 689 and 475 mA h g-1, resp., at a c.d. of 0.5 A g-1.  The Co9S8-carbon composite powders exhibited a stable cyclability with a reversible capacity of 404 mA h g-1 for the 50th cycle and a superior rate capability compared with bare Co1-xS powder.  The improvement of Na-storage performance could be attributed to the small size and entanglement of the Co9S8 nanoparticles within the carbon matrix.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-014-0648-z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choi, S.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na Storage Capability Investigation of a Carbon Nanotube-Encapsulated Fe1-xS Composite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Ying; Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Belharouak, Ilias; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

When tested in the full cell, a capacity of 438.5 mAh/g with capacity retention of 85.0% is manifested after 80 cycles based on the mass of the anode.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly stable supercapacitors with MOF-derived Co9S8/carbon electrodes for high rate electrochemical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Shuo; Li, Daohao; Chen, Shuai; Yang, Xianfeng; Zhao, Xiaoliang; Zhao, Quansheng; Komarneni, Sridhar; Yang, Dongjiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Co9S8 has received intensive attention as an electrode material for elec. energy storage (EES) systems due to its unique structural features and rich electrochem. properties.  However, the instability and inferior rate capability of the Co9S8 electrode material during the charge/discharge process has restricted its applications in supercapacitors (SCs).  Here, MOF-derived Co9S8 nanoparticles (NPs) embedded in C co-doped with N and S (Co9S8/NS-C) were synthesized as a high rate capability and super stable electrode material for SCs.  The Co9S8/NS-C material was characterized by XRD, XPS, SEM, and high-resoln. TEM (HRTEM).  The Co9S8/NS-C material possessed a unique nanostructure in which Co9S8 NPs were encapsulated in porous graphitic C co-doped with N and S.  The N/S co-doped porous graphitic C of composite led to improved rate performance by enhancing the stability of the electrode material and shortening the ion diffusion paths due to a synergistic effect.  The as-prepd. Co9S8/NS-C-1.5 h material exhibited a high specific capacitance of 734 F g-1 at a c.d. of 1 A g-1, excellent rate capability (653 F g-1 at 10 A g-1) and superior cycling stability, i.e., capacitance retention of ∼99.8% after 140,000 cycles at a c.d. of 10 A g-1.  Thus, a new approach to fabricate promising electrode materials for high-performance SCs is presented here.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2017.08.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sheet-on-sheet chrysanthemum-like C/FeS microspheres synthesized by one-step solvothermal method for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sheet-on-sheet chrysanthemum-like C/FeS microspheres synthesized by one-step solvothermal method for high-performance sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Zhenjiang; Song, Huaihe; Cao, Bin; Ma, Jing; Chen, Xiaohong; Zhou, Jisheng; Ma, Zhaokun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
